                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The chromosome-scale reference genome of black pepper provides insight into piperine biosynthesis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-10-16"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Black pepper (Piper nigrum), dubbed the &#8216;King of Spices&#8217; and &#8216;Black Gold&#8217;, is one of the most widely used spices. Here, we present its reference genome assembly by integrating PacBio, 10x Chromium, BioNano DLS optical mapping, and Hi-C mapping technologies. The 761.2&#8201;Mb sequences (45 scaffolds with an N50 of 29.8&#8201;Mb) are assembled into 26 pseudochromosomes. A phylogenomic analysis of representative plant genomes places magnoliids as sister to the monocots-eudicots clade and indicates that black pepper has diverged from the shared Laurales-Magnoliales lineage approximately 180 million years ago. Comparative genomic analyses reveal specific gene expansions in the glycosyltransferase, cytochrome P450, shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase, lysine decarboxylase, and acyltransferase gene families. Comparative transcriptomic analyses disclose berry-specific upregulated expression in representative genes in each of these gene families. These data provide an evolutionary perspective and shed light on the metabolic processes relevant to the molecular basis of species-specific piperine biosynthesis. Black pepper (Piper nigrum) belongs to the long-isolated lineage of basal angiosperm and its fruit has been used for food spice and phytomedicines for thousands of years. Here, the authors assemble the reference genome of this species and analyze gene families associated with piperine biosynthesis."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Black pepper (<i>Piper nigrum</i>), dubbed the âKing of Spicesâ and âBlack Goldâ, is one of the most widely used spices. Here, we present its reference genome assembly by integrating PacBio, 10x Chromium, BioNano DLS optical mapping, and Hi-C mapping technologies. The 761.2âMb sequences (45 scaffolds with an N50 of 29.8âMb) are assembled into 26 pseudochromosomes. A phylogenomic analysis of representative plant genomes places magnoliids as sister to the monocots-eudicots clade and indicates that black pepper has diverged from the shared Laurales-Magnoliales lineage approximately 180 million years ago. Comparative genomic analyses reveal specific gene expansions in the glycosyltransferase, cytochrome P450, shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferase, lysine decarboxylase, and acyltransferase gene families. Comparative transcriptomic analyses disclose berry-specific upregulated expression in representative genes in each of these gene families. These data provide an evolutionary perspective and shed light on the metabolic processes relevant to the molecular basis of species-specific piperine biosynthesis.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Black pepper (<i>Piper nigrum</i>, 2nâ=â52), known as the âKing of Spicesâ, is one of the oldest and most widely used seasonings in the world. It had been highly valued and considered as âBlack Goldâ, occupying a preeminent status in the spice trade. It was the primary spice in early trading between Europe and Asia. The production, transportation and consumption of black pepper has influenced the destinies of nations and their people, both economically and culturally<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Ravindran, P. Black pepper: Piper nigrum (CRC Press, 2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d32293e720">1</a></sup>. Originating in the humid, tropical evergreen forests of the Western Ghats of South India, black pepper is now cultivated in most tropical and subtropical regions, with production primarily in Vietnam, Indonesia, Brazil, India, Sri Lanka, China, Malaysia and Cambodia. In 2017, the worldâs total cultivation area was 458,731 hectares, which gives 510,045 metric tons of production and 2 billion US dollar trade value. It is also an important cash crop for small farmers in many developing countries.</p><p>Black pepper is a perennial, woody climbing vine. It belongs to the family Piperaceae, which is the largest family in the order Piperales. In current phylogenetic classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. et al. Phylogeny and Evolution of the Angiosperms: Revised and Updated Edition (University of Chicago Press, 2018)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d32293e727">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d32293e727_1">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sauquet, H. &amp; MagallÃ³n, S. Key questions and challenges in angiosperm macroevolution. New Phytologist 219, 1170â1187 (2018)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d32293e727_2">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chase, M. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Botanical J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d32293e730">5</a></sup>, Piperales is considered as a sister order of the Canellales, Laurales and Magnoliales orders within the Magnoliid clade. However, the phylogenetic position of magnoliids relative to eudicots and monocots is still unsettled, even based on the two newly published magnoliid genomes, which indicates a controversial phylogenetic understanding of these long-isolated lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e734">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S.-M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63â73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d32293e734_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6â7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d32293e737">8</a></sup>.</p><p>The Piperales are well known for their special phytochemistry, particularly their unique piperidine alkaloids. Piperine is the major alkaloid responsible for the pungency and flavour of black pepper. In addition to being a common culinary spice and a preservative for meat products, black pepper has been widely used in traditional medicinal systems, such as the Indian Ayurvedic system, traditional Chinese medicine and folklore medicines of Latin America and Southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Meghwal, M. &amp; Goswami, T. K. Piper nigrum and Piperine: an update. Phytother. Res. 27, 1121â1130 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d32293e744">9</a></sup>. Piperine possesses a range of pharmacological activities, including the attenuation of fat cell differentiation through the downregulation of peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor (PPAR) gamma expression, leading to its use for treatment of diabetes as a PPAR agonist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kharbanda, C. et al. Novel piperine derivatives with antidiabetic effect as PPARâÎ³ agonists. Chem. Biol. Drug Des. 88, 354â362 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d32293e748">10</a></sup>. It has also been used as an antioxidant, antitumour, antimicrobial, anti-depressive and anti-inflammatory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Manayi, A., Nabavi, S. M., Setzer, W. N. &amp; Jafari, S. Piperine as a potential anti-cancer agent: a review on preclinical studies. Curr. medicinal Chem. 25, 4918â4928 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d32293e752">11</a></sup> agent. The phytochemical and pharmacological characteristics of black pepper have received renewed attention in recent decades. However, relatively little is understood about the genetic mechanisms controlling of its biosynthetic pathway and accumulation, and few genetic resources are available for black pepper.</p><p>Here, we report a reference genome of black pepper using a combination of four technologies. Evolutionary analysis of three available magnoliid genomes from different orders provides evidence for the phylogenetic position of the magnoliids. In addition, our comparative genome and transcriptome analyses identify changes in gene expression, evolution and family size associated with piperine biosynthesis. Genomic resources provided here will be valuable for biological and agronomic research in <i>Piper</i> species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and main features</h3><p>Cv. Reyin1 derived from the cultivar âLampung Daun Kecilâ was used for genome sequencing. Based on the k-mer genome survey analysis (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">1</a>), black pepper (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">1</a>) was estimated to have a genome size of 761.74âMb. K-mer analysis with a length of 17 indicates the genome had high heterozygosity (1.33%) and a repetitive sequence content of 59.54% (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><p>To overcome the impact of heterozygosity and repetitive sequence content on the construction of a chromosome-scale reference genome, a comprehensive de novo assembly strategy (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">2</a>) combining Illumina paired-end reads (137âÃâcoverage), PacBio single-molecule long reads (N50 length of 13 Kb, ~138âÃâcoverage), 10X Genomics, BioNano (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">2</a>), and Hi-C sequencing (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">3</a>) was adopted. The workflow is summarised as follows: (1) FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d32293e795">12</a></sup> was selected for the PacBio-only assembly, using the overlap-layout-consensus algorithm and FALCON-Unzip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d32293e799">12</a></sup> for true diploid assembly; (2) fragScaff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Adey, A. et al. In vitro, long-range sequence information for de novo genome assembly via transposase contiguity. Genome Res. 24, 2041â2049 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32293e804">13</a></sup>, which leverages information derived from different barcoded pools, was used to order and orient linked contigs into longer scaffolds, which resulted in an assembly (Piper_nigrum_v1) consisting of 1277 scaffolds with an N50 of 2.3âMb and a total length of 791.0âMb; (3) a non-haplotype-aware hybrid assembly with âno extend splitâ and âno cut segdupsâ parameters (according to BioNanoâs suggestions) was performed using BioNano Solve tools, yielding an assembly with a total molecule length of 316,350.85âMb and 128âÃâeffective coverage (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">2</a>). This improved version (Piper_nigrum_v2) contains 201 hybrid scaffolds with an N50 of 7.8âMb (3.4 fold improvement compared with the Piper_nigrum_v1) and a longest scaffold of 25.8âMb (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">3</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">5</a>); (4) additional scaffold refinement was performed in a Hi-C experiment with ~125 million unique Di-Tags read pairs (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">6</a>) and postprocessing by gap filling and polishing to generate the final version of the assembly, âPiper_nigrum_v3â. This final assembly contains only 45 scaffolds, with a scaffold N50 of 29.8âMb and 99.9% of the assembled genome contained in 26 scaffolds (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Tab1">1</a>). Inasmuch as the chromosome number of <i>Piper nigrum</i> is 2nâ=â52<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Samuel, R. &amp; Morawetz, W. Chromosomal evolution within Piperaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 166, 105â117 (1989)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d32293e830">14</a></sup>, we infer that these large scaffolds reflect a chromosome-scale assembly (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">7</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">14</a>). The assembly size of 761.22âMb (99.93% coverage of the genome) was very similar to the estimated genome size of 761.74âMb obtained from the k-mer analysis. The higher heterozygosity estimated from the k-mer analysis was also consistent with single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) calling in the final reference genome (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Major indicators of the <i>Piper nigrum</i> genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Detailed assembly data are summarised in TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Tab1">1</a>. The extent of comprehensive gene coverage was assessed by screening for 248 core eukaryotic genes (CEGs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1026">15</a></sup>, which revealed a complete and partial matches for 234 (94.35%) and 244 (98.39%) genes, respectively (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">4</a>). BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1033">16</a></sup> analysis against the plant-specific database containing a total of 430 genes revealed 413 (96.1%) complete BUSCOs, 80 (19.1%) of which were duplicated genes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">5</a>). These data support the interpretation that the <i>P. nigrum</i> genome assembly is reasonably complete.</p><p>A total of 54.85% repetitive sequences were identified in the black pepper genome. Among these repeats, 54.01% are classified as interspersed repeats (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">7</a>). Similar to most plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Kim, N.-S. The genomes and transposable elements in plants: are they friends or foes? Genes Genom. 39, 359â370 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1050">17</a></sup>, the predominant type of transposable elements (TEs) was long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, accounting for 40.55% of the genome, including 27.63% LTR/Gypsy and 9.95% LTR/Copia retro-elements (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">8</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">16</a>). These TEs exhibit an apparently random distribution on the chromosomes, and an inverse correlation with gene density (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig1">1</a>; Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">17</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">25</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div <b>e</b>â<b>l</b> Expression of berry-specific genes (from outside to inside tracks: 2 MAP, 4 MAP, 6 MAP, 8 MAP, root, stem, leaf and flower). <b>m</b> Locations of genes mapped to secondary Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We conducted Illumina strand-specific RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) using eight different tissues and organs, and performed PacBio isoform sequencing (Iso-Seq) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">26</a>) to provide transcriptional evidence supporting the annotation and to obtain reliable gene structure annotation. We also employed a strategy that combined ab initio and evidence-based gene prediction using the BRAKER2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hoff, K. J., Lange, S., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. BRAKER1: unsupervised RNA-Seq-based genome annotation with GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS. Bioinformatics 32, 767â769 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1123">18</a></sup> pipeline. The black pepper genome encodes 63,466 inferred protein-coding genes, with an average length of 900âbp and an average GC content of 51.21%. Annotation Edit Distance (AED)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Yandell, M. &amp; Ence, D. A beginnerâs guide to eukaryotic genome annotation. Nat. Rev. Genet. 13, 329â342 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1127">19</a></sup> quantification showed a high AED of 0.04 at the nucleotide-level and 0.13 at the exon level, indicating a highly credible gene model.</p><p>Five thousand eighty-two transcription factors (TFs) from 75 gene families accounting for 8.0% of the protein-coding genes are categorised in this report. In addition, 646 chromatin regulators (CRs), 157 transcriptional regulators (TRs), 6509 long non-coding RNAs (lncRNAs), 1514 tRNAs, 1206 rRNAs, 1533 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) and 256 microRNAs (miRNAs) were also identified (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">27</a>). An InterProScan Pfam analysis identified 3652 protein families containing 21,184 proteins and 2071 Gene Ontology (GO) terms, of which 41.63%, 13.19% and 45.18% of the genes were annotated in the biological process, cellular component and molecular function categories, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">28</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomic and phylogenomic analyses</h3><p>Ancient whole-genome duplication (WGD) (also known as polyploidization) events are important driving forces of the evolution of animals, fungi and other organisms, particularly plant lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Adams, K. Genomic clues to the ancestral flowering plant. Science 342, 1456â1457 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1151">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1154">21</a></sup>. We selected a range of species to perform a comparative genomic investigation and assess WGD in black pepper: <i>Papaver somniferum</i> (WGD ~7.8 million years ago (MYA))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science 362, 343â347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1161">22</a></sup>; <i>Liriodendron chinense</i> as a representative of the Magnoliidae, with a WGD event ~116 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1168">6</a></sup>; <i>Coffea canephora</i> as a representative of whole-genome triplication (WGT) of the eudicots (WGT-Î³) without WGT-1 and WGT-2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181â1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1176">23</a></sup>; <i>Helianthus annuus</i> for its lineage-specific WGD-2 with shared ancestral WGT-Î³ and WGT-1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148â152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1183">24</a></sup>; and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> which represents the closest modern chromosome relative of the ancestral eudicot karyotype (AEK) with seven protochromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1190">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Salse, J. Ancestors of modern plant crops. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 134â142 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1193">26</a></sup>. The reciprocal best hit (RBH) gene pair synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) distribution (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">29</a>) recovered the WGT-Î³ in <i>Coffea canephora</i> and a relatively recent WGD event in <i>Helianthus annuus</i> and <i>Papaver somniferum</i>, consistent with the findings of previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148â152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1215">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liu, X. et al. The genome of medicinal plant Macleaya cordata provides new insights into benzylisoquinoline alkaloids metabolism. Mol. Plant 10, 975â989 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1218">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jaramillo, M. A. &amp; Manos, P. S. Phylogeny and patterns of floral diversity in the genus Piper (Piperaceae). Am. J. Bot. 88, 706â716 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1221">28</a></sup>. Indeed, the all-vs-all paralog analysis in black pepper genome detected 31,138 RBH paralogous gene pairs and the RBH paralog <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution showed a single peak at ~ 0.1 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2b</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">29a</a>). Second, the synteny dot plot analysis revealed duplications within the black pepper genome that are either inter-chromosomal or intra-chromosomal duplications (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">30</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12607-6/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12607_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12607-6/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12607_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="404" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Comparative genomic analysis of black pepper and WGD. <b>a</b> Sharing of gene families by black pepper and five other species. The numbers indicate gene families identified among all selected species. <b>b</b> Synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) distributions of syntenic blocks for <i>Piper nigrum</i> paralogs and orthologs with other eudicots are shown by coloured lines, as indicated. <b>c</b> Phylogenetic tree with 82 single-copy orthologs from 21 species identified by OrthoMCL to show divergence times. <i>Piper nigrum</i> was placed sister to MagnolialesâLaurales among the magnoliids. Posterior probabilities for all branches exceed 0.99. Divergence times were estimated using BEAST and are indicated by light blue bars at the internodes with 95% highest posterior density (HPD). Ð Cambrian, O Ordovician, S Silurian, D Devonian, C Carboniferous, P Permian, T Triassic, J Jurassic, K Cretaceous, Pg Paleogene, N Neogene. The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2</a>a and b are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>An analysis of the genomic synteny of black pepper using MCScanX revealed 1295 syntenic blocks across the whole genome including 28,621 genes that accounted for 45.10% of the total number of genes. Among these syntenic blocks, 855 (66.0%) of the paralogous gene pairs were located inter-chromosomally, and the other 440 (34.0%) were located within chromosomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig1">1</a>). In addition, an analysis of the type of duplication of the black pepper paralogs using MCScanX indicated that most genes were classified as WGDs or segmental duplications (32,547 genes, accounting for 51.3%), followed by three other types: dispersed (19.1%), proximal (7.4%) and tandem (3.6%) duplications. We also performed a comparative genomic analysis of black pepper with <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i> and identified 1:1 and 1:2 syntenic depth ratios in the <i>Amborella</i>-<i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i> and <i>Amborella</i>-<i>Piper nigrum</i> comparisons (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">31a</a>), respectively. Furthermore, an analysis of synteny in black pepper with <i>Amborella</i> showed 316 syntenic blocks that covered 40% and 34% of the assembled genomes, respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">31b</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">10</a>). By calculating the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> of the black pepper syntenic block gene pairs, a major peak was detected at ~0.1 (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">29b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">32</a>).</p><p>The RBH and syntenic block gene pair <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution (Supplementary Note 4) provided convincing evidence for a WGD event during black pepper genome evolution. Based on the slow substitution rate of basal angiosperms, we speculate that the black pepper WGD event (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>â=â0.106âÂ±â0.002) occurred ~17.2â17.9 MYA with a synonymous substitution rate of 3.02Eâ9 synonymous substitutions per year<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738â749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1339">29</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><p>The high-quality reference genome for black pepper enabled us to perform comparative genomics among relatively early diverging angiosperms. We compared the black pepper genome with the genomes of nine eudicots, three monocots, three magnoliids, <i>Amborella</i>, two gymnosperms and two species <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> and <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> as the outgroups.</p><p>Eighty-two single-copy orthologous gene families among the 21 species were identified using OrthoMCL to accomplish this goal, and a phylogenetic analysis of these families using BEAST placed magnoliids as a sister clade to the monocotsâeudicots clade, consistent with <i>Liriodendron</i> genome research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1365">6</a></sup>, the Plastid Phylogenomic Angiosperm (PPA) tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, H.-T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461â470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1369">30</a></sup> and Angiosperm Phylogeny Group (APG) IV<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chase, M. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Botanical J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1373">5</a></sup>. Furthermore, <i>Piper nigrum</i> was placed as a sister to MagnolialesâLaurales among the magnoliids (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2c</a>), consistent with its phylogenetic position inferred from chloroplast genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhou, J. et al. Molecular structure and phylogenetic analyses of complete chloroplast genomes of two Aristolochia medicinal species. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 18, 1839 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1384">31</a></sup>. Based on our results, Piperales, representated by <i>Piper nigrum</i>, first diverged from the Magnoliales (<i>Liriodendron chinense</i>) plus Laurales (<i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i> and <i>Persea americana</i>) approximately 175â187 MYA (95% highest posterior density (HPD) interval).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Evolution piperine biosynthesis-associated genes</h3><p>Piperine is synthesised from two direct precursor substrates, piperoyl-coenzyme A and piperidine, in a reaction catalysed by an acyltransferase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Geisler, J. G. &amp; Gross, G. G. The biosynthesis of piperine in Piper nigrum. Phytochemistry 29, 489â492 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1408">32</a></sup>. Thus, piperine production is associated with three major gene groups (see below): group I: genes in the phenylpropanoid pathway (KEGG pathway: map00940), which produce cinnamoyl-CoA for piperoyl-coenzyme A biosynthesis via a few complex reactions, such as amino transfer and elimination of ammonia-lyase and cinnamate 4-hydroxylation; group II: genes involved in L-lysine metabolism (KEGG pathway: map01064), which catalyse the transformation of lysine to piperidine via a series of reactions including decarboxylation, amine oxidation, cyclization and reduction; and group III: acyltransferase genes, that catalyse the synthesis of piperine in the presence of piperoyl-coenzyme A and piperidine.</p><p>We acquired insights into the genomic basis of piperine biosynthesis, using OrthoMCL to identify orthologous genes and CAFE to identify gene family clusters, with a specific focus on the expansion (gain) and contraction (loss) events related to the above three gene groups in black pepper. This process identified 39,400 gene families consisting of 471,854 genes among the 21 species (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2a</a>; for clarity, only species with a close evolutionary relationship to black pepper are shown). Four hundred twenty-three gene families containing 4228 genes were unique to black pepper.</p><p>According to the CAFE analysis, 91 gene clusters (including 21,368 genes) and 189 gene clusters (including 14,201 genes) expanded and contracted, respectively, in the black pepper genome. Among these clusters, 35 and 4 families exhibited significant (family-wide <i>p</i>-valueââ¤â0.01) contractions and expansions, respectively (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig3">3</a>). Furthermore, the species-specific expanded genes were significantly enriched in two main types, one of which was genes with secondary metabolite-associated functions, such as glycosyltransferases (<i>GTFs</i>; 19 genes), shikimate hydroxycinnamoyl transferases (<i>HCTs</i>; 69 genes), cytochrome P450s (<i>CYP</i>; 31 genes), lysine decarboxylases (<i>LDCs</i>; 75 genes), BAHD acyltransferases (<i>BAHD-ATs</i>; 6 genes) and serine carboxypeptidase-like acyltransferases (<i>SCPL-ATs</i>; 7 genes) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4a</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">33</a>). These expanded genes occurred in gene clusters on some chromosomes (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">34a</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">f</a>). A KEGG pathway analysis of these specific gene families revealed marked enrichment in genes involved in piperine synthesis, including the biosynthesis of alkaloids from the shikimate pathway (map01063); alkaloids from ornithine, lysine and nicotinic acid (map01064); phenylpropanoid biosynthesis (map00940); and phenylalanine, tyrosine and tryptophan biosynthesis (map00400). The other category was disease resistance-related genes, such as disease-resistance proteins (RPS5, RPP13, RGA1, RGA2, RGA3, RGA4 and RGA5), NBS-LRR disease-resistance proteins, LRR receptor-like serine/threonine protein kinases (EFR, BAM1, FLS2, GSO2 and EMS1) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">34</a>âg, âh) and salicylic acid-binding protein 2. These genes have been widely reported to play important roles in pathogen-resistance mechanisms. The expansion of these genes might indicate that biotic stresses from pathogen infection and animal ingestion are a source of major selection pressure on black pepper in the tropical rainforest.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12607-6/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12607_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-12607-6/MediaObjects/41467_2019_12607_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="353" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Estimation of gene family expansion and contraction on each evolutionary branch.
