The source data underlying Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4aâh</a> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Upregulation of piperine biosynthesis-related genes</h3><p>The piperine content determined using high-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">5</a>) revealed that the organ with the highest content was berry at 8 months after pollination (MAP), followed by 6 MAP, 4 MAP, 2 MAP, flower, root, stem and leaf (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>). A transcriptomic analysis was performed with RNA-seq using samples from different organs of black pepper, including berries (which synthesise piperine) at different developmental stages (2 MAP, 4 MAP, 6 MAP and 8 MAP; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">35</a>), and organs not containing piperine, i.e., the stem, root, leaf and flower (Supplementary Note 6 and Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">36</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">38</a>), to investigate genes involved in piperine production. A gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Subramanian, A. et al. Gene set enrichment analysis: a knowledge-based approach for interpreting genome-wide expression profiles. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 15545â15550 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1601">33</a></sup> and weighted gene co-expression network analysis (WGCNA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1605">34</a></sup> revealed that upregulated genes in berries relative to nonberry organs were associated with the phenylpropanoid pathway (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">39</a>) and lysine metabolism. The transcriptional module (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">40</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">42</a>) of piperine synthesis in berries exhibited significant enrichment of the amino transferase (<i>PPA-AT</i>), arogenate dehydratase (<i>ADT</i>), cinnamate 4-hydroxylase (<i>C4H</i>/<i>CYP73</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ehlting, J., Hamberger, B., Million-Rousseau, R. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. Cytochromes P450 in phenolic metabolism. Phytochemistry Rev. 5, 239â270 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1630">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Batard, Y. et al. Regulation of the cinnamate 4-hydroxylase (CYP73A1) in jerusalem artichoke tubers in response to wounding and chemical treatments. Plant Physiol. 113, 951â959 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1633">36</a></sup>), <i>HCT</i>, <i>p</i>-coumarate 3-hydroxylase (<i>C3H</i>), caffeic acid-3-O-methyltransferase (<i>CAOMT</i>), <i>p</i>-coumarate-CoA-ligase (<i>4CL</i>), <i>GTF, CYP72</i> and <i>CYP71</i> clans (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">43</a>). These genes are involved in the metabolism of phenylpropanoids to produce piperoyl-CoA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Laurent, H., Stephane, M., Francoise, M., Pierrette, G. &amp; Michel, L. Purification, cloning, and properties of an acyltransferase controlling shikimate and quinate ester intermediates in phenylpropanoid metabolism. J. Biol. Chem. 278, 95â103 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1669">37</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>). In addition, the differential expression of <i>LDC</i>, primary-amine oxidase (<i>PAO</i>) and diamine oxidase (<i>DAO</i>) in black pepper berries (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>) is consistent with the hypothesis that lysine, rather than quinolizidine or indolizidine, is the intermediate precursor of piperidine synthesis in black pepper. Importantly, the expansion of the <i>BAHD-AT</i> and <i>SCPL-AT</i> gene families (11 <i>BAHD-ATs</i> and 3 <i>SCPL-ATs</i>) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4a</a>), accompanied by the high transcriptional activity of these genes in berries (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>), is associated with the metabolism of phenylpropanoids and lysine into piperine (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Purifying selection of piperine biosynthesis gene families</h3><p>Based on our analysis of gene expansion and expression, we synthetically investigated the gene-sequence-level features of the aforementioned three gene groups exhibiting expansion and berry-specific upregulated expression that might contribute to the unique piperine production in black pepper.</p><p>Within group I, the <i>GTF</i>, <i>CYP</i> and <i>HCT</i> gene families were significantly expanded in black pepper (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4a</a>). <i>HCT</i> has been reported to play a crucial role in piperoyl-CoA synthesis (a precursor for piperine biosynthesis) by catalysing the transformation of <i>p</i>-coumaroyl-CoA into <i>p</i>-coumaroyl shikimic acid and caffeoyl shikimic acid into caffeoyl-CoA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Hoffmann, L. et al. Silencing of hydroxycinnamoyl-coenzyme A shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyltransferase affects phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. Plant Cell 16, 1446â1465 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1745">38</a></sup>. The <i>HCT</i> gene family was expanded in the black pepper genome (69 genes in black pepper compared with one or two genes in the other species). The particularly high expression in berries (2 MAP, 6 MAP and 8 MAP; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>) detected by analysing transcriptomes from different organs is consistent with a role for this family in the biosynthesis of piperine. In the sequence-level analysis of <i>HCT</i> expansion, most conserved regions (107 sites) were under strong purifying selection, with one site showing diversifying selection and eight sites showing episodic selection (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4c,</a> d). The adaptive expansion of <i>HCT</i> and the maintenance of duplicates appear to have operated in concert, resulting in higher enzyme levels for the accumulation of the necessary donor precursor used for piperine biosynthesis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>). Despite the significant expansion of the <i>GFT</i> and <i>CYP</i> gene families in black pepper, no positively selected sites were detected (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">44a,</a> b). The significant expansion of <i>GFT</i>, <i>CYP</i> and <i>HCT</i> family genes suggests the importance of the phenylpropanoid pathway, which provides precursors for the biosynthesis of alkaloids and flavonoids typical of modern pepper cultivars (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>).</p><p>Within group II, LDC catalyses the first step in lysine metabolism (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>) and serves as the rate-limiting enzyme in the synthesis of lysine-derived alkaloids, including piperidine alkaloids (piperine), quinolizidine alkaloids and indolizidine alkaloids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bunsupa, S. et al. Lysine decarboxylase catalyzes the first step of quinolizidine alkaloid biosynthesis and coevolved with alkaloid production in leguminosae. Plant Cell 24, 1202â1216 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1796">39</a></sup>. Seventy-five <i>LDC</i> genes were detected in black pepper, compared with just six in <i>Ananas comosus</i>, five in <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and at most one in the other species studied (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4a</a>). An analysis of sequence evolution in the <i>LDC</i> family indicated that 19 sites have been under strong purifying selection, two sites have been under diversifying selection and five sites under episodic selection (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4e</a>, f). The expansion of <i>LDC</i> genes suggests the unique activation of lysine-derived alkaloid synthesis in black pepper.</p><p>Within group III, <i>BAHD-AT</i> and <i>SCPL-AT</i> encode the two main acyltransferase families that use phenolic compounds as donor molecules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bontpart, T., Cheynier, V., Ageorges, A. &amp; Terrier, N. BAHD or SCPL acyltransferase? What a dilemma for acylation in the world of plant phenolic compounds. New Phytologist 208, 695â707 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1831">40</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>). The <i>BAHD-AT</i> and <i>SCPL-AT</i> genes were expanded to include six and seven members, respectively, in black pepper. An assessment of sequence evolution revealed that 261 sites have been under strong purifying selection, one site has been under diversifying selection and nine sites under episodic selection in <i>BAHD-ATs</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4g,</a> h), and no sites showed positive selection in <i>SCPL-ATs</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">45</a>). This adaptive expansion may have resulted from purifying and diversifying selection and led to high levels of <i>BAHD-AT</i> and <i>SCPL-AT</i> transcripts in the berries (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4b</a>), further explaining why piperine is uniquely accumulated in black pepper.</p><p>Overall, the main pathway leading to piperine synthesis and key genes involved in its regulation (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>) are consequences of the expansion of multiple gene families, followed by evolutionary selection leading to transcription specifically in the berries. However, we fully recognise that a direct causal link between each identified gene and metabolite abundance remains to be established. Nevertheless, the genetic framework underpinning the evolution of piperidine alkaloid biosynthesis is being clarified.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The Piperales represents a useful taxonomic group for the study of the link between evolution, ecology and phytochemistry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Dyer, L. A. &amp; Palmer, A. D. Piper: A Model Genus for Studies of Phytochemistry, Ecology, and Evolution (Springer, 2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1883">41</a></sup> because of its geographic distribution pattern, the diversity of its lineages, and its characteristic secondary metabolism. The black pepper genome will provide resources for phylogenomic analyses and studies of piperine synthesis. Piperales is one of the most diverse lineages of basal angiosperms. In terms of stem anatomy, <i>Piper nigrum</i> has a pattern of vascular bundle arrangement that is similar to monocots, whereas the vascular bundle arrangement of <i>Piper colubrinum</i> is similar to eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Empari, K. &amp; Sim, S. In National Conference on Pepper in Malaysia (Kuching, Sarawak (Malaysia), Universiti Pertanian Malaysia,Â 16â17 December 1985)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1893">42</a></sup>. In addition, the cotyledonary forms of <i>Peperomia pellucida</i>, <i>Peperomia peruviana</i> and <i>Peperomia parvifolia</i> show an evolution typical of eudicots to monocots. Interestingly, <i>Liriodendron</i> (Magnoliales) also shows features of both monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1910">6</a></sup>. Because of their antiquity and morphological diversity, the phylogenetic positions of various lineages within the magnoliid clade have remained unclear, with three primary proposed tree topologies reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1914">6</a></sup>. Two newly issued genome sequences have been used to address the phylogenetic position of magnoliids, but have produced conflicting results<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1918">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chaw, S.-M. et al. Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution. Nat. Plants 5, 63â73 (2019)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1918_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6â7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1921">8</a></sup>. In our comparative genome analysis, a combination of three representative orders (Piperales, Magnoliales and Laurales) of magnoliids indicates that magnoliids are sisters to the monocotsâeudicots clade, consistent with research into the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, J. et al. Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and speciesâpair differentiation. Nat. Plants 5, 18â25 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1929">6</a></sup>, the PPA tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, H.-T. et al. Origin of angiosperms and the puzzle of the Jurassic gap. Nat. Plants 5, 461â470 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1933">30</a></sup> and APG IV<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chase, M. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Botanical J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1â20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1937">5</a></sup>. In addition, <i>Piper nigrum</i> is placed as a sister to MagnolialesâLaurales among the magnoliids (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig2">2c</a>). According to Soltis et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Nuclear genomes of two magnoliids. Nat. Plants 5, 6â7 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d32293e1948">8</a></sup>, the inclusion of complete angiosperm lineages that were missing in the present study (Nymphaeales, Austrobaileyales, Chloranthales, Ceratophyllales and Canellales in magnoliids) will facilitate a better understanding of the phylogenetic relationships of these diverse and long-isolated lineages of flowering plants.</p><p>The current assembly of the black pepper genome provides insights into the underpinning genetic changes following WGD events that are responsible for the unique biosynthesis of piperine. Our current study has focused on analysing the biological processes related to piperine biosynthesis and provides useful information on the significant expansion of gene families that are involved in piperine synthesis. Most notable is the discovery of the berry-specific expression of a series of relevant genes, including <i>LDC</i> genes involved in lysine metabolism; <i>GTF</i>, <i>CYP</i> and <i>HCT</i> genes in the phenylpropanoid pathway, and <i>BAHD-AT</i> and <i>SCPL-AT</i> genes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4a</a>). The phenylpropanoid, amino acid pathways and <i>ATs</i> are ubiquitous in plant secondary metabolism. <i>Coffea canephora</i>, <i>Malus domestica</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Camellia sinensis</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i> and <i>Citrus sinensis</i> are rich in derivatives of phenylpropanoids, and these species synthesise secondary metabolites through the convergence of the two metabolic pathways described above. One example is <i>Capsicum</i> (chili pepper) species, in which capsaicinoids are derived from the phenylpropanoid and branched-chain fatty acid pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Aza-GonzÃ¡lez, C., NÃºÃ±ez-Palenius, H. G. &amp; Ochoa-Alejo, N. Molecular biology of capsaicinoid biosynthesis in chili pepper (Capsicum spp.). Plant Cell Rep. 30, 695â706 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2005">43</a></sup>. In addition, the precursors of nicotine alkaloids in tobacco (<i>Nicotiana</i>) species are derived from terpenoid and amino metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="The Tomato Genome, C. et al. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2013">44</a></sup>. Lysine-derived quinoline alkaloids are synthesised through the convergence of phenylpropanoid and lysine metabolism in <i>Nelumbo nucifera</i>, <i>Papaver somniferum</i>, <i>Macleaya cordata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liu, X. et al. The genome of medicinal plant Macleaya cordata provides new insights into benzylisoquinoline alkaloids metabolism. Mol. Plant 10, 975â989 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2025">27</a></sup> and <i>Carica papaya</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bennett, R. N., Kiddle, G. &amp; Wallsgrove, R. M. Biosynthesis of benzylglucosinolate, cyanogenic glucosides and phenylpropanoids in Carica papaya. Phytochemistry 45, 59â66 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2032">45</a></sup>. However, piperine synthesis originates from the decarboxylation and amine oxidation of lysine, which distinguishes it from the polymerisation of two tyrosines in benzylisoquinoline alkaloid biosynthesis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>). Finally, two precursors derived from phenylpropanoid and lysine metabolism are catalysed by acyltransferase to produce piperine, and the associated genes exhibit gene expansion and high, berry-specific transcriptional activity in black pepper. The convergence of phenylpropanoid and lysine metabolism, specifically the decarboxylation and amine oxidation of lysine, and acyl transformation represent the characteristic features of piperine synthesis (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#Fig4">4i</a>), and we describe the genetic and evolutionary basis of these features in this study.</p><p>Sequencing the black pepper genome has advanced our understanding of the unique piperine biochemistry of black pepper. Our study therefore provides valuable insights that may serve as a foundation for future research on Piperales taxonomy and piperine biosynthesis, leading to a better understanding of the evolution, phytochemistry and ecology of the <i>Piper</i> genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Leaf sample collection and DNA library construction</h3><p>Fresh leaf tissues from single-living black pepper plants were collected to extract genomic DNA and RNA (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">1</a>). For genome survey analysis, a short paired-end Illumina DNA library with a 350âbp insert size (137âÃâcoverage) was sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer. For PacBio Sequel sequencing, 50âÂµg of high-molecular-weight (HMW) genomic DNA were prepared to generate five standard SMRTbell libraries with 20âKb insertions. PacBio long reads were sequenced using 15 SMRTcells on the PacBio Sequel System (Pacific Biosciences) with SMRTbell Template Prep Kit 1.0-SPv3 (Pacific Biosciences). HMW genomic DNA was also prepared for 10âÃâGenomics libraries according to the manufacturerâs protocol (Chromium Genome v1, PN-120229). Sequencing-read libraries were sequenced using HiSeq 2500 with 2âÃâ150 paired-end reads to generate ~96âGb (120âÃâcoverage) raw data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Transcriptome library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>RNA-seq experiments (three biological replicates) used RNA extracted from different organs of the black pepper Cv. Reyin1 (root, stem, leaf, flower and berries at four different stages: 2 months after pollination (MAP), 4 MAP, 6 MAP and 8 MAP) (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">35</a>). Prepared libraries were sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform according to the manufacturerâs recommended protocol. We generated an average of 28.0 million paired-end reads for each sample.</p><p>RNA samples from black pepper Cv. Reyin1 leaves were also prepared for full-length transcriptome sequencing using the PacBio Iso-Seq protocol. The synthetic full-length cDNAs were selected to prepare a 20âKb SMRTbell Template library for sequencing on a PacBio Sequel instrument.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Preprocessing of PacBio Iso-Seq reads</h3><p>The PacBio Iso-Seq3 pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/IsoSeq3">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/IsoSeq3</a>) was applied to obtain high-confidence transcriptome reads through the CCS (circular consensus sequence), classify, cluster and polishing process. The high-quality, full-length and consistent isoform transcript sequences were prepared for subsequent analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">De novo genome assembly</h3><p>The errors in the PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequences were initially corrected using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722â736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2100">46</a></sup> with the default parameters. Because of heterozygosity and repeated sequences, FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050â1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2104">12</a></sup> was subsequently employed for de novo assembly using the corrected reads to produce primary contigs (p-Contigs). Then, FALCON-Unzip used the p-Contigs to perform phasing and directional classification of the heterozygosity from the initial assembly into updated primary Contigs (p-Contigs) and haplotigs (h-Contigs). Finally, the postprocessing step was used to polish using Arrow<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2108">47</a></sup> based on corrected PacBio long reads. The 10X Genomics Linked-reads were mapped to the consensus assembly described above using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv Preprint arXiv: 1303.3997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2112">48</a></sup> with a default parameter. Then, fragScaff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Adey, A. et al. In vitro, long-range sequence information for de novo genome assembly via transposase contiguity. Genome Res. 24, 2041â2049 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2116">13</a></sup> was used to extend contigs into initiatory scaffolds (Piper_nigrum_v1) according to the recommended scripts and processes of the fragScaff software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">BioNano optical maps and hybrid assembly</h3><p>Total of 750âng of fresh young leaf tissues were collected from living plant material following BioNano Genomics guidelines. HMW genomic DNA with a fragment distribution ranging from 150 Kb to 2âMb was fluorescently labelled using single-sequence-specific DLE-1 endonucleases (BioNano Genomics) based on the BioNano Direct Label and Stain (DLS) technology. The fluorescently labelled DNA was stained for at least two hours at room temperature and then loaded onto a Saphyr chips to scan on the BioNano Genomics Saphyr System by the sequencing provider Berry Genomics Corporation.</p><p>BioNano data were first filtered based on molecule length, mapping rate and label density using BioNano Solve (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/10/30182-Bionano-Tools-Installation-Guide.pdf">https://bionanogenomics.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/10/30182-Bionano-Tools-Installation-Guide.pdf</a>). Non-haplotype de novo assembly was performed in BioNano Solve using filtered high-quality BNX files based on the OverlapâLayoutâConsensus paradigm. The 350,823 filtered DLE-1 molecules (N50 length 0.288âMb) produced 547 genome maps with an N50 of 3.8âMb for a total map length of 1304âMb. The de novo assembly containing BioNano molecules was combined with Piper_nigrum_v1 input into the BioNano Solve hybrid scaffolding pipeline to produce a hybrid scaffold assembly with -T <i>p</i>-value set to 1e-10 in both initial alignment, final alignment and hybrid scaffold steps, and more stringent value of 1eâ11 for both the âmerge_Tvalueâ and âT_cutoffâ <i>p</i>-value thresholds. Finally, the hybrid assembly had an N50 of 7.8âMb, and the longest scaffold was 25.8âMb. This stage of assembly was term Piper_nigrum_v2 (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Scaffolding with Lachesis</h3><p>About 5âg of fresh young leaf tissue from living plants were macerated and crosslinked using paraformaldehyde to capture the interacting DNA segments. Chromatin was subsequently digested with HindIII (NEB), and biotinylated nucleotides were used to fill in the resulting sticky ends. Following ligation, a protease was used to remove the crosslinks. Finally, genomic DNA was extracted, sheared into 350âbp fragments using a focused ultrasonicator (Covaris, Woburn, USA), and fragments into which biotin had been incorporated were pulled down with streptavidin-coated magnetic beads based. Purified DNA was then prepared and sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq instrument according to the manufacturerâs recommendations.</p><p>Hi-C paired-end reads were trimmed to remove low-quality bases and Illumina adapter sequences using Trimmomatic (<a href="http://www.usadellab.org/cms/?page=trimmomatic">http://www.usadellab.org/cms/?pageâ=âtrimmomatic</a>), and then checked with HiCUP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wingett, S. et al. HiCUP: pipeline for mapping and processing Hi-C data. F1000res. 4, 1310 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2166">49</a></sup>. The scaffolding began by aligning the clean Hi-C read pairs to Piper_nigrum_v2 using the aln and sampe commands from BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv Preprint arXiv: 1303.3997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2170">48</a></sup> with default parameters. Then, Lachesis (<a href="https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS">https://github.com/shendurelab/LACHESIS</a>) with the cluster number set to 26 and other parameters set to the default values were used to cluster, order and orient the scaffolds. The oriented scaffolds were used to build the interaction matrices with juicer (<a href="https://github.com/aidenlab/juicer">https://github.com/aidenlab/juicer</a>), inspect and manually correct with Juicebox assembly tools (<a href="https://github.com/aidenlab/Juicebox">https://github.com/aidenlab/Juicebox</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Post-processing</h3><p>We also performed an additional round of gap filling to eliminate the gaps generated in the final scaffolding steps using PBJelly (<a href="https://github.com/alvaralmstedt/Tutorials/wiki/Gap-closing-with-PBJelly">https://github.com/alvaralmstedt/Tutorials/wiki/Gap-closing-with-PBJelly</a>) with PacBio subreads. The assembled scaffolds were further polished with Pilon (<a href="https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon">https://github.com/broadinstitute/pilon</a>) using Illumina paired-end reads to correct base errors. The ultimate assembly consisted of 45 scaffolds with a total size of 761.2âMb and a scaffold N50 of 29.8âMb. This assembly is designated as final version of black pepper genome: Piper_nigrum_v3.</p><p>For an assessment of completeness, the Piper_nigrum_v3 genome was subjected to a BUSCO analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2221">16</a></sup> and compared with the Viridiplantae_odb10 database (Update date: 2017â12â01) with the --long parameter. In addition, Core Eukaryotic Genes (CEGs) were also aligned to Piper_nigrum_v3 using CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2225">15</a></sup> with the default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genomic annotation</h3><p>We used a combination of the de novo repeat library and homology-based strategies to identify repeat structures. TransposonPSI (<a href="http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net/">http://transposonpsi.sourceforge.net/</a>) was used to identify transposable elements; GenomeTools suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinform. 10, 645â656 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2244">50</a></sup> (LTRharvest and LTRdigest) was used to annotate LTR-RTs with protein HMMs from the Pfam (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">6</a>) database (Supplementary Note 2). Then, a de novo repeat library of black pepper was built using RepeatModeler software (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>), and each of the three repeat libraries was classified with RepeatClassifier, followed by Merge and de-redundancy using USEARCH (<a href="https://www.drive5.com/usearch/">https://www.drive5.com/usearch/</a>) withââ¥â80% identity. Subsequently, the non-redundant repeat library was analysed using BLASTX to search the transposase database (-evalue 1e-10) and non-redundant plant protein databases (-evalue 1e-10) to remove protein-coding genes. Unknown repetitive sequences were further classified used CENSOR (<a href="https://www.girinst.org/censor/index.php">https://www.girinst.org/censor/index.php</a>). Then, the de novo repeat library was used to discover and mask the assembled genome with RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RMDownload.html</a>) with the -xsmall parameter.</p><p>For gene structure annotations, the Illumina RNA-seq data from black pepper Cv. Reyin1 were aligned to repeat-softmasked genome using GSNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Nacu, S. Fast and SNP-tolerant detection of complex variants and splicing in short reads. Bioinformatics 26, 873â881 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2283">51</a></sup>, which generates intron hints with other aligned hints (ESTs, proteins and nucleotides from NCBI) for gene structure annotation. For PacBio Iso-Seq reads, GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859â1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2287">52</a></sup>, a splice-aware aligner, was used to align the high-quality isoform transcripts to the repeat-softmasked genome for the detection of new isoforms.</p><p>The structural annotation of protein-coding genes was performed using BRAKER2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hoff, K. J., Lange, S., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M. &amp; Stanke, M. BRAKER1: unsupervised RNA-Seq-based genome annotation with GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS. Bioinformatics 32, 767â769 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2294">18</a></sup>, which integrates GeneMark-ET and AUGUSTUS by combining the aligned resulted from ab initio predictions, homologous protein mapping, RNA-seq mapping and GMAP PacBio mapping to produce the final gene set. Gene models from these different approaches were combined using the EVM software (version 1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2298">53</a></sup>. Moreover, tRNA loci, rRNA, lncRNAs, snRNA and miRNAs and non-protein-coding genes were annotated by performing homologous searching and deep-learning analyses across the assembled genome sequence (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Functional annotation of protein-coding genes</h3><p>Predicted genes were subjected to functional annotation by performing a BLASTP homologue search against the UniProtKB Viridiplantae database, and the NCBI non-redundant protein database with an e-value threshold of 1e-10. In addition, a comprehensive annotation was also achieved using InterProScan (v5.31â70.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2313">54</a></sup>, which includes motifs/domains prediction, functional classifications, protein family identification, transmembrane topology, predicted signal peptides and GO annotations. KAAS (<a href="https://www.genome.jp/kegg/kaas/">https://www.genome.jp/kegg/kaas/</a>) and KOBAS 3.0 (<a href="http://kobas.cbi.pku.edu.cn/">http://kobas.cbi.pku.edu.cn/</a>) were used to search the KEGG GENES database for KO (KEGG Orthology) assignments and generating a KEGG pathway membership. PlantTFcat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Dai, X., Sinharoy, S., Udvardi, M. &amp; Zhao, P. X. PlantTFcat: an online plant transcription factor and transcriptional regulator categorization and analysis tool. Bmc Bioinforma. 14, 321 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2331">55</a></sup> was also used to systematically analyse InterProScan domain and categorise possible chromatin regulators (CRs), transcription factors (TFs) and other transcriptional regulators (TRs) in the current assembly. The plantiSMASH version 3.0.5-a04b4cd<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Kautsar, S. A., Suarez Duran, H. G., Blin, K., Osbourn, A. &amp; Medema, M. H. plantiSMASH: automated identification, annotation and expression analysis of plant biosynthetic gene clusters. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, W55âW63 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2335">56</a></sup> software was used to cluster the plant-specific secondary metabolism genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Transcriptome assembly and gene expression analysis</h3><p>RNA-seq reads from three replicates of the eight tissue types were preprocessed using Trimmomatic by removing adaptor sequences and filtering low-quality reads. HISAT2 was used to align the samples with the genome for genome-guided transcript assembly using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650â1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2347">57</a></sup>.</p><p>Read counts extracted from StringTie were filtered using the sva R package to decrease batch effects and hidden variables. Differentially expressed genes (DEGs) were detected using DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2354">58</a></sup>, and calculated based on absolute log2 transformed fold-change values greater than 2 and <i>p</i>-value of 0.05 using the BenjaminiâHochberg correction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. B 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2361">59</a></sup>. A gene set enrichment analysis (GSEA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Subramanian, A. et al. Gene set enrichment analysis: a knowledge-based approach for interpreting genome-wide expression profiles. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 15545â15550 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2365">33</a></sup> was performed to determine significant gene sets, and the WGCNA package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Langfelder, P. &amp; Horvath, S. WGCNA: an R package for weighted correlation network analysis. BMC Bioinforma. 9, 559 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2369">34</a></sup> in R was applied to perform a multivariate analysis of gene co-expression modules. See Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">6</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Synteny analyses</h3><p>For estimation of the time of whole-genome duplication events, BLASTP reciprocal best hit (RBH) pairwise sequences of paralogous (within the species genome) and orthologous relationships (between black pepper and other species) were identified and the synonymous divergence levels (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) were calculated using the YN model in KaKs_Calculator v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genomics Proteom. Bioinforma. 8, 77â80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2390">60</a></sup>. The raw <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions were used to fit a mixture model of Gaussian distributions and thus derive the mean <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values between paralogs and orthologues in R package Mclust 5. The <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions of orthologues between black pepper and other species were adopted to compare the relative substitution rates in different species by plotting with the ggplot2 package. The divergence time of black pepper was calculated by combining the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> value with synonymous substitutions at each site per year (<i>r</i>) for magnoliids through the formula: divergence date (<i>T</i>)â=â<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>/<i>2r</i>. Synteny and syntenic block gene pair analyses were performed using MCScanX and the CoGe Comparative Genomics Platform (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Comparative genomics for phylogenomic and gene family</h3><p>Putative orthologous genes were constructed from nine eudicots, three monocots, three magnoliids, <i>Amborella</i>, two gymnosperms and the outgroups <i>Selaginella moellendorfii</i> and <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> were inferred using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, L., Jr, S. C. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2445">61</a></sup> and compared with protein genes from the current assembly genome of black pepper to investigate the evolution and phylogenetic placement of black pepper among seed plants (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">7</a>). The total of one-to-one orthologous gene sets were identified and extracted for alignment using muscle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2453">62</a></sup> and further trimmed using Gblocks 0.91b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Talavera, G. &amp; Castresana, J. Improvement of phylogenies after removing divergent and ambiguously aligned blocks from protein sequence alignments. Syst. Biol. 56, 564â577 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2457">63</a></sup>. A maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree was constructed using concatenated alignment with RAxML v8.2.12<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2461">64</a></sup> and the PROTGAMMAILGF model to automatically determine the best reasonable tree by conducting 1000 bootstrap replicates. The maximum likelihood tree was used as a starting tree to estimate species divergence time using BEAST v2.1.2 (Bayesian Evolutionary Analysis Sampling Trees2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Barido-Sottani, J. et al. Taming the BEAST-a community teaching material resource for BEAST 2. Syst. Biol. 67, 170â174 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2465">65</a></sup>. A Calibrated Yule model with a Strict Clock rate and gamma hyperparameter of prior distribution were used to estimate the divergence time. Speciation event dates for <i>Ananas comosus</i>-<i>Oryza sativa</i> (Normal model, Mean: 105 MYA, Sigma: 0.5) and <i>Liriodendron chinense</i>-<i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i> (Normal model, Mean: 118 MYA, Sigma: 0.5), which were obtained using Timetree web service (<a href="http://www.timetree.org/">http://www.timetree.org/</a>), <i>Papaver somniferum</i>-<i>Nelumbo nucifera</i> (Normal model, Mean: 125 MYA, Sigma: 0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Guo, L. et al. The opium poppy genome and morphinan production. Science 362, 343â347 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2495">22</a></sup>, monocotsâeudicots (Log Normal model, Mean: 150 MYA, Std dev: 4MYA), were used to calibrate the divergence time. The Markov chain MonteâCarlo analysis was repeated 10,000,000 times with 1000 steps.</p><p>The orthologous genes and phylogenetic tree topology inferred from the OrthoMCL analysis were taken into CAFE v4.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, (1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2502">66</a></sup>, which employed a random birth and death model, to estimate the size of each family at each ancestral node and obtain a family-wise <i>p</i>-value (based on a MonteâCarlo re-sampling procedure) to indicate whether has a significant expansion or contraction occurred in each gene family across species</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Identification of alkaloid and piperine biosynthesis genes</h3><p>Predicted protein-coding genes from the black pepper genome were aligned against annotated alkaloid and piperine biosynthesis pathway genes in the KEGG database by BLASTP with an e-value cut-off of 1e-5, identityâ&gt;â50% and alignment coverageââ¥â50%. In addition, all orthologous gene sequences involved in alkaloid and piperine biosynthesis were also captured from comparative genomics analyses, and corresponding gene functions were verified by searching the published literature and the NCBI and UniProt databases.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Sequence evolution of gene families</h3><p>Gene families derived from OrthoMCL identification were aligned using ClustalW (<a href="http://www.clustal.org/clustal2">http://www.clustal.org/clustal2</a>) and trimmed with trimAl (v1.2) (<a href="http://trimal.cgenomics.org/introduction">http://trimal.cgenomics.org/introduction</a>). Evidence of selection across genes within gene families was tested using a multispecies alignment in HyPhy with the datamonkey webserver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Delport, W., Poon, A. F., Frost, S. D. &amp; Kosakovsky Pond, S. L. Datamonkey 2010: a suite of phylogenetic analysis tools for evolutionary biology. Bioinformatics 26, 2455â2457 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2539">67</a></sup>. The genetic algorithm recombination detection method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Kosakovsky Pond, S. L., Posada, D., Gravenor, M. B., Woelk, C. H. &amp; Frost, S. D. W. GARD: a genetic algorithm for recombination detection. Bioinformatics 22, 3096â3098 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2543">68</a></sup> was performed to detect breakpoints at nucleotide sites. Evidence of positive selection and episodic selection sites in genes was inferred by estimating the rates of synonymous and non-synonymous changes at each site in the aligned sequence through single likelihood ancestor counting (SLAC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Kosakovsky Pond, S. L. &amp; Frost, S. D. W. Not so different after all: a comparison of methods for detecting amino acid sites under selection. Mol. Biol. Evolution 22, 1208â1222 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2547">69</a></sup> and a mixed effects model of episodic diversifying selection (MEME)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Murrell, B. et al. Detecting individual sites subject to episodic diversifying selection. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002764 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d32293e2552">70</a></sup> (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The data sets generated and analysed during this study are available from the corresponding author upon request. All the raw sequencing data generated during this study have been deposited at NCBI as a BioProject under accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA529758">PRJNA529758</a>. Transcriptome sequence reads have been deposited in the SRA database under BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA529760">PRJNA529760</a>. The genome assemblies and annotation files are available at the website <a href="http://cotton.hzau.edu.cn/EN/download.php">http://cotton.hzau.edu.cn/EN/download.php</a>. The source data underlying Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM4">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM4">2</a>a, b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM4">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-12607-6#MOESM4">4aâh</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>
